<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomic diversifications of five Gossypium allopolyploid species and their impact on cotton improvement"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2020 52:5"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-04-20"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Polyploidy is an evolutionary innovation for many animals and all flowering plants, but its impact on selection and domestication remains elusive. Here we analyze genome evolution and diversification for all five allopolyploid cotton species, including economically important Upland and Pima cottons. Although these polyploid genomes are conserved in gene content and synteny, they have diversified by subgenomic transposon exchanges that equilibrate genome size, evolutionary rate heterogeneities and positive selection between homoeologs within and among lineages. These differential evolutionary trajectories are accompanied by gene-family diversification and homoeolog expression divergence among polyploid lineages. Selection and domestication drive parallel gene expression similarities in fibers of two cultivated cottons, involving coexpression networks and N6-methyladenosine RNA modifications. Furthermore, polyploidy induces recombination suppression, which correlates with altered epigenetic landscapes and can be overcome by wild introgression. These genomic insights will empower efforts to manipulate genetic recombination and modify epigenetic landscapes and target genes for crop improvement. Sequencing and genomic diversification of five allopolyploid cotton species provide insights into polyploid genome evolution and epigenetic landscapes for cotton improvement."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Polyploidy is an evolutionary innovation for many animals and all flowering plants, but its impact on selection and domestication remains elusive. Here we analyze genome evolution and diversification for all five allopolyploid cotton species, including economically important Upland and Pima cottons. Although these polyploid genomes are conserved in gene content and synteny, they have diversified by subgenomic transposon exchanges that equilibrate genome size, evolutionary rate heterogeneities and positive selection between homoeologs within and among lineages. These differential evolutionary trajectories are accompanied by gene-family diversification and homoeolog expression divergence among polyploid lineages. Selection and domestication drive parallel gene expression similarities in fibers of two cultivated cottons, involving coexpression networks and <i>N</i><sup>6</sup>-methyladenosine RNA modifications. Furthermore, polyploidy induces recombination suppression, which correlates with altered epigenetic landscapes and can be overcome by wild introgression. These genomic insights will empower efforts to manipulate genetic recombination and modify epigenetic landscapes and target genes for crop improvement.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Polyploidy or whole-genome duplication provides genomic opportunities for evolutionary innovations in many animal groups and all flowering plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Muller, H. Nat. 59, 346â353 (1925)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1086">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, D. Bot. 101, 1057â1078 (2014)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1086_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wendel, J. Plant Biol. 58, 377â406 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1089">5</a></sup>, including most important crops such as wheat, cotton and canola or oilseed rape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chen, Z. J. et al. Toward sequencing cotton (Gossypium) genomes. Plant Physiol. 145, 1303â1310 (2007)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1093">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1096">8</a></sup>. The common occurrence of polyploidy may suggest its advantage and potential for selection and adaptation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Soltis, D. E., Visger, C. J. &amp; Soltis, P. S. The polyploidy revolution thenâ¦and now: Stebbins revisited. Am. J. Bot. 101, 1057â1078 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1100">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J. F. The wondrous cycles of polyploidy in plants. Am. J. Bot. 102, 1753â1756 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1103">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature 543, 346â354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1106">9</a></sup>, through rapid genetic and genomic changes as observed in newly formed <i>Brassica napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1119">11</a></sup> and polyploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Feldman, M. et al. Rapid elimination of low-copy DNA sequences in polyploid wheat: a possible mechanism for differentiation of homoeologous chromosomes. Genetics 147, 1381â1387 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1123">12</a></sup>, and/or largely epigenetic modifications as in <i>Arabidopsis</i> and cotton polyploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Chen, Z. J. Genetic and epigenetic mechanisms for gene expression and phenotypic variation in plant polyploids. Annu. Rev. Plant Biol. 58, 377â406 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1130">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Ding, M. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Epigenetic perspectives on the evolution and domestication of polyploid plants and crops. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 42, 37â48 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1133">13</a></sup>. Cotton is a powerful model for revealing genomic insights into polyploidy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Wendel, J. F. The wondrous cycles of polyploidy in plants. Am. J. Bot. 102, 1753â1756 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1137">3</a></sup>, providing a phylogenetically defined framework of polyploidization (~1.5âmillion years ago (Ma))<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wendel, J. F. &amp; Grover, C. E. in Cotton 2nd edn (eds Fang, D. D. &amp; Percey, R. G.), Vol. 57, 25â44 (Agronomy Monograph 57, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1141">14</a></sup>, followed by natural diversification and crop domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Splitstoser, J. C., Dillehay, T. D., Wouters, J. &amp; Claro, A. Early pre-Hispanic use of indigo blue in Peru. Sci. Adv. 2, e1501623 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1146">15</a></sup>. The evolutionary history of the polyploid cotton clade is longer than that of some other allopolyploids, such as hexaploid wheat (~8,000âyears)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Feldman, M. et al. Rapid elimination of low-copy DNA sequences in polyploid wheat: a possible mechanism for differentiation of homoeologous chromosomes. Genetics 147, 1381â1387 (1997)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1150">12</a></sup>, tetraploid canola (~7,500âyears)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lu, K. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals Brassica napus origin and genetic loci involved in its improvement. Nat. Commun. 10, 1154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1154">16</a></sup> and tetraploid <i>Tragopogon</i> (~150âyears)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chester, M. et al. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1161">11</a></sup>. Polyploidization between an A-genome African species (<i>Gossypium arboreum</i> (Ga)-like) and a D-genome American species (<i>G. raimondii</i> (Gr)-like) in the New World created a new allotetraploid or amphidiploid (AD-genome) cotton clade (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1a</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wendel, J. F. &amp; Grover, C. E. in Cotton 2nd edn (eds Fang, D. D. &amp; Percey, R. G.), Vol. 57, 25â44 (Agronomy Monograph 57, 2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1175">14</a></sup>, which has diversified into five polyploid lineages, <i>G. hirsutum</i> (Gh) (AD)<sub>1</sub>, <i>G. barbadense</i> (Gb) (AD)<sub>2</sub>, <i>G. tomentosum</i> (Gt) (AD)<sub>3</sub>, <i>G. mustelinum</i> (Gm) (AD)<sub>4</sub> and <i>G. darwinii</i> (Gd) (AD)<sub>5</sub>. <i>G. ekmanianum</i> and <i>G. stephensii</i> are recently characterized and closely related to Gh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Grover, C. E. et al. Re-evaluating the phylogeny of allopolyploid Gossypium L. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 92, 45â52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1212">17</a></sup>. Gh and Gb were separately domesticated from perennial shrubs to become annualized Upland and Pima cottons<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Splitstoser, J. C., Dillehay, T. D., Wouters, J. &amp; Claro, A. Early pre-Hispanic use of indigo blue in Peru. Sci. Adv. 2, e1501623 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1216">15</a></sup>. To date, global cotton production provides income for ~100 million families across ~150 countries, with an annual economic impact of ~US$500âbillion worldwide<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, Z. J. et al. Toward sequencing cotton (Gossypium) genomes. Plant Physiol. 145, 1303â1310 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1220">6</a></sup>. However, cotton supply is reduced due to aridification, climate change and pest emergence. Future improvements in cotton and sustainability will involve use of the genomic resources and gene-editing tools becoming available in many crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bevan, M. W. et al. Genomic innovation for crop improvement. Nature 543, 346â354 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1225">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Bailey-Serres, J., Parker, J. E., Ainsworth, E. A., Oldroyd, G. E. D. &amp; Schroeder, J. I. Genetic strategies for improving crop yields. Nature 575, 109â118 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1228">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Eshed, Y. &amp; Lippman, Z. B. Revolutions in agriculture chart a course for targeted breeding of old and new crops. Science 366, eaax0025 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1231">19</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Sequencing features of five cotton allotetraploid species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Sequencing features of five cotton allotetraploid species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="656" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Evolution and domestication of five polyploid lineages, Gh, Gb, Gt, Gd and Gm, after polyploidization between an A-genome African species (Ga-like) and a D-genome American species (Gr-like). Typical seeds from each species are shown. The divergence time estimates are based on 21,567 single orthologs among the 5 species by using the synonymous substitution rate (<i>r</i>) of 3.48âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Scale bar, 10âmm; ka, thousand years ago. <b>b</b>, Chromosomal features and synteny of the Gm genome. Notes in circos plots: (i) estimated lengths of 13 A and 13 D homoeologous pseudochromosomes; (ii) distribution of annotated genes; (iii) TE content (<i>Gypsy</i>, steel blue; <i>Copia</i>, grey; other repeats, orange); (iv,v) stacked SNP (iv) and indel (v) densities between Gm and Gb, Gd, Gh and Gt, respectively (see inset), and (vi) syntenic blocks between the homoeologous A and D chromosomes. The densities in plots in (ii)â(v) are represented in 1âMb with overlapping 200-kb sliding windows. <b>c</b>, Genome-wide syntenic relationships among A and D subgenomes in five allotetraploids relative to the A-genome-like Ga (A<sub>2</sub> genome) and D-genome-like Gr (D<sub>5</sub> genome). Structural variations among syntenic blocks are marked with colored ribbons.</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM4">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Cotton genomes have been sequenced for the D-genome (Gr)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1280">20</a></sup> and A-genome (Ga)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1284">21</a></sup> diploids and two cultivated tetraploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1288">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224â229 (2019)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1288_1">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of cultivated Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum TM-1) provides insights into genome evolution. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 524â530 (2015)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1288_2">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1288_3">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Liu, X. et al. Gossypium barbadense genome sequence provides insight into the evolution of extra-long staple fiber and specialized metabolites. Sci. Rep. 5, 14139 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1291">26</a></sup>. These analyses have shown structural, genetic and gene expression variation related to fiber traits and stress responses in cultivated cottons, but the impact of polyploidy on selection and domestication among the wild and cultivated polyploid cotton species remains poorly understood<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, Z. J. et al. Toward sequencing cotton (Gossypium) genomes. Plant Physiol. 145, 1303â1310 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1295">6</a></sup>. Here we report high-quality genomes for all five allotetraploid species and show that despite wide geographic distribution and diversification, allotetraploid cotton genomes retained the syntenic gene content and genomic diversity relative to respective extant diploids. Evolutionary rate heterogeneities, gene loss and positively selected genes characterize the two subgenomes of each species but differ among polyploid lineages. Transposable elements (TEs) are dynamically exchanged between the two subgenomes, facilitating genome-size equilibration following allopolyploidy. Gene expression diversity in the fiber tissues involves selection, coexpression networks and <i>N</i><sup>6</sup>-methyladenosine (m<sup>6</sup>A) RNA modifications. In cultivated polyploid cottons, recombination suppression correlates with DNA hypermethylation and weak chromatin interactions and can be overcome by wild introgression and possibly epigenetic remodeling. The results offer unique insights into polyploid genome evolution and provide valuable genomic resources for cotton research and improvement.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing, assembly and annotation</h3><p>Sequencing of the five allotetraploid cotton genomes entailed using complementary whole-genome shotgun strategies, including sequencing by single-molecule real-time (PacBio SEQUEL and RSII, ~440Ã genome equivalent), Illumina (HiSeq and NovaSeq, ~286Ã) (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1a</a>) and chromatin conformation capture (Hi-C seq) (~326Ã) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a>). Homozygous single nucleotide polymorphisms (SNPs) and insertions/deletions (indels) were also used to correct the consensus sequence (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1b,c</a>). The rate of anchored scaffolds is 97% in Gb and 99% or higher in the other 4 species. Scaffolds were oriented, ordered and assembled into 26 pseudo-chromosomes with very low (0.1â0.8%) gaps (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1d</a>). The assembled genomes range in size from 2.2 to 2.3 gigabase pairs (Gbp; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Tab1">1</a>), slightly smaller than the sum of the two A- and D-genome diploids (1.7/Aâ+â0.8/Dâââ2.5âGbp/AD)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1337">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1340">21</a></sup>. Nearly 73% of the assembled genomes are repeats and TEs (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1e</a>), predominantly in pericentromeric regions in Gm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1b</a>) and the other 4 species (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig5">1</a>). The completeness and contiguity of these genomes compare favorably with Sanger-based sequences of sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1353">27</a></sup> and <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 2184 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1360">28</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Genome assembly and annotation statistics for five allotetraploid cotton species</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The euchromatic sequences of 5 polyploid genomes are complete (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), as supported by BUSCO scores (&gt;97%) and 36,880 (&gt;99%) primary transcripts from the Gr version 2 release<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1533">20</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1b</a>), with the number of protein-coding genes predicted to range from 74,561 (Gb) to 78,338 (Gt; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Tab1">1</a>), which are 3,000â4,000 more than reported in Gh and Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Wang, M. et al. Reference genome sequences of two cultivated allotetraploid cottons, Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Nat. Genet. 51, 224â229 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1543">23</a></sup>. Although the A subgenome (1.7âGbp) is twice the size of the D subgenome (0.8âGbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1548">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1551">21</a></sup>, mirroring the ancestral state of their extant diploids, the two have similar numbers of protein-coding genes (ratio of D/Aâââ1.06; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1f</a>).</p><p>As an indication of the improved contiguity (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), the contig length in the Gh genome increases 6.9-fold with a 7.7-fold reduction in fragmentation (6,733 versus 51,849), compared to the published sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1564">22</a></sup>. The improvement is substantial in the Gb genome with a 15.9-fold reduction in N50 contigs and a 23-fold increase in N50 contig length (from 77.6 to 1,800âkilobase pairs (kb)). Moreover, most quality scores are 2-5-fold higher in the 3 wild polyploid species than in Gh and Gb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Tab1">1</a>).</p><p>Reciprocal 24-nucleotide masking and syntenic analyses show that our Gh and Gb assemblies have ~23- and 2.7-fold more unique sequences, respectively, than the published ones<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1574">22</a></sup> also with variable gap sizes (10â200âkb; Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig6">2a</a>). Some specific genes are present in our annotations and the published data, which are largely related to gene copy number variation (more decreases than increases). Other differences include inversions (132â133âmegabase pairs (Mb)) with two large ones (A06 and D03) present in similar regions of both Gh and Gb<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1581">22</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig6">2b</a>), which could result from errors and/or unresolved alternative haplotypes; these inversions were confirmed using Hi-C data (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig6">2c</a>). Notably, the published Hai7124 strain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1592">22</a></sup> is a Gb local strain that is different from Gb 3-79, and Gh TM-1 strains may vary; these can also contribute to the observed variation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Evolution within and between five polyploids</h3><p>Using the diploid<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1604">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1607">21</a></sup> and 5 polyploid cotton genomes, we estimated divergence at 58â59âMa between <i>Gossypium</i> and its relative <i>Theobroma cacao</i> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig7">3a</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), 4.7â5.2âMa between the extant diploids (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig7">3b</a>), and 1.0â1.6âMa between polyploid and diploid clades. Genome-wide phylogenetic analysis (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig8">4a</a>) supports a monophyletic origin for the five allotetraploid species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Grover, C. E., Grupp, K. K., Wanzek, R. J. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Assessing the monophyly of polyploid Gossypium species. Plant Syst. Evol. 298, 1177â1183 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1630">29</a></sup>. Within the polyploid clade, the highest divergence (~0.63âMa) occurs between Gm and the other 4 species, with the most recent divergence (~0.20âMa) between Gb and Gd. This genomic diversification was accompanied by biogeographic radiation to the Galapagos Islands (Gd), the Hawaiian Islands (Gt), South America (northeastern Brazil) (Gm)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Wendel, J. F., Brubaker, C., Alvarez, I., Cronn, R. &amp; Stewart, J. M. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton. Plant Genetics and Genomics: Crops and Models Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) 3â22 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1634">30</a></sup>, Central and South America, the Caribbean, and the Pacific (Gh and Gb)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Brubaker, C. L., Bourland, F. M. &amp; Wendel, J. F. in Cotton: Origin, History, Technology, and Production (eds Smith, C. W. &amp; Cothren, J. T.) 3â32 (John Wiley &amp; Sons, 1999)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1638">31</a></sup>, with separate distribution and domestication of diploid cultivated cottons in southern Arabia, North Africa, western India and China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kulkarni, V. N., Khadi, B. M., Maralappanavar, M. S., Deshapande L. A. &amp; Narayanan, S. S. in Genetics and Genomics of Cotton. Plant Genetics and Genomics: Crops and Models Vol. 3 (ed. Paterson, A. H.) 69â97 (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1642">32</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig8">4b</a>). Over the last 8,000âyears, Upland (Gh) and Pima (Gb) cottons were independently domesticated in northwest South America and the Yucatan Peninsula of Mexico, respectively, under strong human selection, leading to the modern annualized crops<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Splitstoser, J. C., Dillehay, T. D., Wouters, J. &amp; Claro, A. Early pre-Hispanic use of indigo blue in Peru. Sci. Adv. 2, e1501623 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1650">15</a></sup>.</p><p>After whole-genome duplication, duplicate genes may be lost or diverge in functions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Lynch, M. &amp; Conery, J. S. The evolutionary fate and consequences of duplicate genes. Science 290, 1151â1155 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1657">33</a></sup>, but the pace of this process has rarely been studied in allopolyploids. Using 17,136 homoeolog pairs shared among all 5 allotetraploid species, we demonstrate that most (14,583, 85.5%) homoeolog pairs evolved at statistically indistinguishable rates throughout the polyploid clade relative to the diploids (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">2a</a>), but those with rate shifts occur more commonly in the A (1,476, 8.5%) than in the D (845, 5%) subgenome. We further revealed that the D homoeologs generally acquire substitution mutations more quickly than the A homoeologs in most lineages, whereas the Gh and Gt lineages experience a greater rate of divergence in the A than in the D homoeologs (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">2b</a>). This relative acceleration of A-homoeolog divergence is mirrored in lineage-specific rate tests; the Gh/Gt clade including Upland cotton has the fastest evolving A homoeologs and the slowest evolving D homoeologs among five polyploids. These results demonstrate pervasive lineage-specific rate heterogeneities between subgenomes and among different polyploid cottons.</p><p>We examined patterns of gene loss and gain using 4,369 single-copy orthologs (SCOs), which are present in both diploids and in one or more allotetraploids (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig8">4c</a>). Analysis of gene loss and gain among these basally shared homoeologs in the five polyploid lineages showed the highest level of net gene loss between the initial polyploidization and Gm, with threefold higher levels in the A subgenome (547 net gene losses) than in the D subgenome (149). Other polyploids have fewer gene losses with no subgenomic bias.</p><p>Among the homoeologs shared by all five polyploid species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2a</a>), the number of genes under positive selection (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub> valuesâ&gt;â1) is the highest (3,200â3,300) in Gm with the longest branch relative to others, and the lowest between Gb and Gd (~1,100), the most recently diverged polyploid clade (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">3</a>). Across different polyploid lineages, 10â20% more D homoeologs are under positive selection than A homoeologs, suggesting a concerted evolutionary impact on subgenomic functions in all polyploid species.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Gene family expansion and contraction in cultivated and wild allotetraploid cotton species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Gene family expansion and contraction in cultivated and wild allotetraploid cotton species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="707" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Venn diagram representing the shared orthologous groups (orthogroups) between cotton species. No species-specific orthogroups were identified by Orthofinder (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a>). <b>b</b>, Venn diagram of R-gene family expansion and contraction in these five species. <b>c</b>,<b>d</b>, Flower morphology of two cultivated (<b>c</b>) and three wild (<b>d</b>) polyploid cotton species. Scale bars, 30âmm. <b>e</b>,<b>f</b>, Gene Ontology (GO)-term enrichment of the shared genes in polyploid domesticated cottons (<b>e</b>) and wild species (<b>f</b>).</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM5">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genomic diversity among five polyploids</h3><p>The two subgenomes in each of the five polyploid species are highly conserved at the chromosomal, gene content and nucleotide levels (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1b</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig5">1</a>). The D subgenomes have fewer and smaller inversions than the A subgenomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1c</a>), as reported for Gh<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1753">25</a></sup>, except for a few small inversions in D10 of GtâGm and GmâGb and D12 of GdâGtâGm. This level of structural conservation is similar to some polyploids such as wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium et al. Shifting the limits in wheat research and breeding using a fully annotated reference genome. Science 361, eaar7191 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1757">7</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis suecica</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Novikova, P. Y. et al. Genome sequencing reveals the origin of the allotetraploid Arabidopsis suecica. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 957â968 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1764">34</a></sup>, but is different from others such as <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7908â7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1770">10</a></sup>, peanut<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Bertioli, D. J. et al. The genome sequence of segmental allotetraploid peanut Arachis hypogaea. Nat. Genet. 51, 877â884 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1774">35</a></sup> and <i>T. miscellus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chester, M. et al. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1780">11</a></sup>, which show rapid homoeologous shuffling.</p><p>The genomic conservation is extended to gene order, collinearity and synteny (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1c</a>). Among the annotated genes (74,561â78,338), 56,870 orthologous groups or 65,300 genes (32,650 homoeologous pairs) (84â88%) are shared among all 5 species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1f</a>).</p><p>The number of SNPs is in the range of 4â12 million (1.7â5.2 SNPsâkb<sup>â1</sup>) or 0.19â0.53% among 5 polyploid genomes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Gm has the highest SNP level (0.53%) relative to the other 4 species, with the lowest between the most recently diverged species Gb and Gd (~0.19%). Similar trends of indels range from ~5.55âMb (~0.76%) in GmâGt to ~3.35âMb (~0.34%) in GbâGd (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig5">1</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">5</a>). The level of overall variation of SNPs and indels among cotton species is low, comparable to natural variation (3.5â4.1 SNPsâkb<sup>â1</sup>) between <i>Brachypodium</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Gordon, S. P. et al. Extensive gene content variation in the Brachypodium distachyon pan-genome correlates with population structure. Nat. Commun. 8, 2184 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1819">28</a></sup> but lower than that (~7.4 SNPsâkb<sup>â1</sup>) for subspecies of rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Zhang, J. et al. Extensive sequence divergence between the reference genomes of two elite indica rice varieties Zhenshan 97 and Minghui 63. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, E5163âE5171 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1825">36</a></sup>. SNPs are more frequent in pericentromeric regions, while indel distributions coincide with gene densities (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1b</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig5">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">TE exchanges between two subgenomes that equilibrate the genome-size variation</h3><p>The size difference between the Ga (~1.7âGbp) and Gr (~0.8âGbp)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. Repeated polyploidization of Gossypium genomes and the evolution of spinnable cotton fibres. Nature 492, 423â427 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1844">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Li, F. et al. Genome sequence of the cultivated cotton Gossypium arboreum. Nat. Genet. 46, 567â572 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1847">21</a></sup> genomes is preserved in the respective A and D subgenomes of the 5 allotetraploid species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig3">3a</a>). The A subgenome consists of a substantial amount of repetitive DNA in centromeric and pericentromeric regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig3">3b</a>). However, the A subgenome has 4.0â5.9% lower repetitive DNA content than the A-genome diploid (Ga), whereas the D subgenome has 1.5â2.9% higher content than the D-genome diploid (Gr) in Gh (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig3">3c</a>) and the other 4 species (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig9">5a</a>). Consistently, the D subgenome has 10â20% more long terminal repeat (LTR) TEs than the D-genome diploid, while the A subgenome has 3â11% fewer LTRs than the A-genome diploid. These changes in subgenomic TEs may account for slight genome downsizing (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Tab1">1</a>) and genome-size equilibration following allopolyploidy in all five species, suggesting that the âevolutionary tapeâ is replayed across polyploid lineages.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Genomic diversification of A and D subgenomes in five allotetraploid cotton species."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Genomic diversification of A and D subgenomes in five allotetraploid cotton species.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="543" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Chromosome painting of Gh using DNA probes to label telomeres (green) and 25S ribosomal DNA (red); DNA is stained by 4â²,6-diamidino-2-phenylindole (DAPI, blue). The A (lower half) and D (upper half) homoeologous chromosomes are separated and rearranged into a tree shape. <b>b</b>, Pairwise comparison (dot plots) of 18-nucleotide sequences between the Gh and Gb homoeologous chromosomes D01 (top) and A01 (bottom) using Genome Pair Rapid Dotter (Gepard) plot analysis (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a>). The blue arrowheads indicate approximate centromeric locations. Genomic length positions are shown in each plot. <b>c</b>, Cumulative percentages (<i>y</i> axis) of 20-nucleotide sequences and their frequencies (<i>x</i> axis) in the A and D subgenomes of Gh relative to Ga (A) and Gr (D). <b>d</b>, The divergence time (Ma) of TEs (<i>Copia</i> and <i>Gypsy</i>) in the A and D subgenomes relative to their A- and D-genome-like diploids, Ga.A and Gr.D, respectively. The divergence time was estimated using the synonymous substitution rate (<i>r</i>) of 3.48âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM6">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Copia-</i> and <i>Gypsy</i>-like TEs are the most abundant LTRs in the Gh genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1926">25</a></sup>. Estimates indicate that divergence of 5.6% (Gt) to 15.5% (Gh) and 39.7% (Gb) LTRs occurred during polyploid diversification (&lt;0.6âMa; Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig9">5bâf</a>). Since polyploid formation, LTRs increased substantially in the D subgenome of all five polyploids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig3">3d</a>). The results indicate activation of LTRs in the D subgenome following polyploidization or movement of LTRs from the A to D subgenome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Zhao, X. P. et al. Dispersed repetitive DNA has spread to new genomes since polyploid formation in cotton. Genome Res. 8, 479â492 (1998)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1936">37</a></sup>. Indeed, some <i>Copia-</i> and <i>Gypsy</i>-like elements are present in the D subgenome but absent in the extant D-genome diploid (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig9">5g</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene family diversification</h3><p>The domesticated (Gh and Gb) and wild (Gm, Gt and Gd) cotton species share 417 (403) and 464 (359) unique genes (orthogroups) in respective groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2a</a>), and no species-specific orthogroups are identified, although they possess distinct phenotypic traits such as fiber length (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1a</a>) and flower morphology (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2c,d</a>). The unique genes in the two domesticated cottons are over-represented in biological processes such as microtubule-based movement and lipid biosynthetic process and transport in the domesticated cottons (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2e</a>; <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05), reflecting the traits related to fiber development and cottonseed oil. Moreover, many of these genes are under positive selection and overlap regions of domestication traits including fiber yield and quality in Upland cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Ma, Z. et al. Resequencing a core collection of upland cotton identifies genomic variation and loci influencing fiber quality and yield. Nat. Genet. 50, 803â813 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1975">38</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">6</a>). The unique genes in all three wild polyploid species, however, are enriched for pollination and reproduction (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2f</a>), suggesting a role of these genes in reproductive adaptation in natural environments.</p><p>Plants have evolved an intricate innate immune system to protect them from pathogens and pests through intracellular disease-resistance (R) proteins as a defense response<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jones, J. D. &amp; Dangl, J. L. The plant immune system. Nature 444, 323â329 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d11877e1988">39</a></sup>. Among the R genes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), each species has its unique R genes with very few genes shared between species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">7</a>), despite 5 wild and cultivated species sharing a core R-gene set (271), suggesting extensive diversification of R genes during selection and domestication. This is in contrast to a shared set of unique genes (related to fiber and seed traits) between the two cultivated species and the other shared set (related to reproductive and adaptive traits) among the three wild species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig2">2a</a>)</p><p>Between the two subgenomes, the D subgenome has higher numbers of R genes (7.8%) than does the A subgenome (<i>P</i>â=â0.0126, Studentâs <i>t</i>-test; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">7</a>). Using the published data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Phillips, A. Z. et al. Genomics-enabled analysis of the emergent disease cotton bacterial blight. PLoS Genet. 13, e1007003 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2020">40</a></sup>, we found expression induction of ~96% of 291 and 384 predicted R genes in the A and D subgenomes, respectively, by bacterial blight pathogens; 19 in D and 7 in A are upregulated at significant levels (error corrected, FDRâ=â0.05 and <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001, exact test), while a similar trend of R-gene expression is observed after the reniform nematode attack (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">8</a>), suggesting a contribution of the D-genome species to disease-resistance traits.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Gene expression diversity</h3><p>In the five allotetraploid species sequenced, gene expression diversity is dynamic and pervasive across developmental stages and between subgenomes (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">9</a>). Principal component analysis shows clear separation of expression between developmental stages (PC1) and between subgenomes (PC3; Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig10">6a</a>), with more D homoeologs expressed than A homoeologs in most tissues examined (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig11">7</a>), consistent with higher levels of tri-methylation of Lysâ4 on histone H3 (H3K4me3) in the former than in the latter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Zheng, D. et al. Histone modifications define expression bias of homoeologous genomes in allotetraploid cotton. Plant Physiol. 172, 1760â1771 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2048">41</a></sup>. Notably, expression correlates more closely with the subgenomic variation than with tissue types, except for fiber elongation and cellulose biosynthesis, where subgenomic expression patterns are more closely correlated between Upland and Pima cottons (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig10">6b</a>). This may suggest that domestication drives parallel expression similarities of fiber-related genes in the two cultivated species.</p><p>These differentially expressed genes in fibers may contribute to fiber development, as they show enrichment of GO groups in hydrolase and GTPase-binding activities (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig12">8a,b</a>). Hydrolases are essential for plant cell wall development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Schroder, R., Atkinson, R. G. &amp; Redgwell, R. J. Re-interpreting the role of endo-beta-mannanases as mannan endotransglycosylase/hydrolases in the plant cell wall. Ann. Bot. 104, 197â204 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2061">42</a></sup>, and Ras and Ran GTPases are implicated in the transition from primary to secondary wall synthesis in fibers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Trainin, T., Shmuel, M. &amp; Delmer, D. P. In vitro prenylation of the small GTPase Rac13 of cotton. Plant Physiol. 112, 1491â1497 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2065">43</a></sup>. Moreover, translation and ribosome biosynthesis pathway genes are enriched during fiber elongation in Upland cotton and during cellulose biosynthesis in Pima cotton, consistent with faster fiber development in Upland cotton and longer fiber duration in Pima cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Tuttle, J. R. et al. Metabolomic and transcriptomic insights into how cotton fiber transitions to secondary wall synthesis, represses lignification, and prolongs elongation. BMC Genomics 16, 477 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2069">44</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Expression networks and m<sup>6</sup>A RNA in fibers</h3><p>Gene expression diversity is also reflected by coexpression modules in fibers among four species (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). These module-related genes show higher semantic similarities between domesticated cottons (GhâGb) than with two wild species (Gt and Gm). The modules include supramolecular fiber organization genes in Upland cotton and brassinosteroid signaling genes in Pima cotton, which could affect fiber cell elongation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Sun, Y. et al. Brassinosteroid regulates fiber development on cultured cotton ovules. Plant Cell Physiol. 46, 1384â1391 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2090">45</a></sup>. The two wild species have different biological functions and transcription factors enriched in fiber-related gene modules (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">11</a>), which may account for the fiber traits that are very different from those of the domesticated species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><p>Transcriptional and post-transcriptional regulation, including the activity of small RNAs and DNA methylation, mediates fiber cell development<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Song, Q., Guan, X. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Dynamic roles for small RNAs and DNA methylation during ovule and fiber development in allotetraploid cotton. PLoS Genet. 11, e1005724 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2103">46</a></sup>. Modification of m<sup>6</sup>A messenger RNA can stabilize mRNA and promote translation with a role in developmental regulation of plants and animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Shen, L., Liang, Z., Wong, C. E. &amp; Yu, H. Messenger RNA modifications in plants. Trends Plant Sci. 24, 328â341 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2109">47</a></sup>. In Upland cotton, m<sup>6</sup>A peaks are found largely in the 5Ê¹ and 3Ê¹ untranscribed regions (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig12">8c</a>) of 1,205 genes in developing fibers (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">12</a>), at levels 7-fold more than in leaves (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig12">8d</a>) (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.002, Studentâs <i>t</i>-test), while the number of expressed genes is similar in both tissues. Notably, both m<sup>6</sup>A-modified mRNAs and transcriptome data in the fibers target the genes involved in translation, hydrolase activity and GTPase-binding activities (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig12">8a</a>). These results indicate that mRNA stability and translational activities may determine fiber elongation and cellulose biosynthesis when cell cycles arrest in fiber cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Recombination and epigenetic landscapes</h3><p>Polyploidy leads to low genetic recombination, as observed in <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Cifuentes, M. et al. Repeated polyploidy drove different levels of crossover suppression between homoeologous chromosomes in Brassica napus allohaploids. Plant Cell 22, 2265â2276 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2146">48</a></sup>, which may comprise bottlenecks for breeding improvement. To determine the recombination landscapes in polyploid cottons, we genotyped 17,134 SNPs using the new Gh sequence and the CottonSNP63K array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Hinze, L. L. et al. Diversity analysis of cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.) germplasm using the CottonSNP63K Array. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 37 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2150">49</a></sup> and identified a total of 1,739 low-recombination haplotype blocks (cold spots) in Upland cotton using whole-genome population-based linkage analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559â575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2154">50</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). These blocks (average ~678.9âkb with 8.4âSNPs) span 1.18âGbp (~52%) of the genome, including ~58% and ~41% in the A and D subgenomes, respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig4">4a</a>), and are dispersed among all chromosomes with large ones predominately near pericentromeric regions. Recombination is generally suppressed throughout haplotype blocks, in contrast to that in subtelomeric regions (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig13">9a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Low-recombination haplotype blocks and their stability and selection during breeding and domestication."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Low-recombination haplotype blocks and their stability and selection during breeding and domestication.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="838" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Distribution of presence (red) or absence (blue) of low-recombination haplotype blocks (red) in each pseudo-molecule of the A (A01âA13) and D (D01âD13) subgenomes in Gh. Map positions (Mb) are indicated in the <i>y</i> axis. <b>b</b>, A low-recombination haplotype block near the pericentromeric region (~72âMb) of chromosome A08 (bottom). The color indicates the coefficient of linkage disequilibrium (<i>D</i>â²) from low (blue) to high (red) with the upper confidence bound (<i>D</i>â²â=â0.90) for the recombination cutoff. The recombination rates (<i>y</i> axis; using locally estimated scatterplot smoothing (LOESS) regression, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Sec12">Methods</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>) in Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub> (blue), Gm Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (green), Gt Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (yellow) and the consensus (red) are shown above with the positions (Mb, <i>x</i> axis). Two elevated recombination events are detected in Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub> (blue arrows) and one in Gm Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (green arrow). <b>c</b>, The average percentage (%) of CG (circle), CHG (triangle) and CHH (cross) methylation in the recombination hotspots (red) and cold spots (blue) between Gb and Gh.
