The CHH methylation is clustered in the left lower corner, which is visible in an enlarged image (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10a</a>).</p><p><a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM7">Source data</a></p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Chromosome A08 has 62 haplotype blocks, including an exceptionally large one (~72âMb) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig4">4b</a>). Interestingly, interspecific hybridization between different tetraploids can increase recombination rates in these regions. For example, in the Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub> population, recombination rates increased more than 4â6âcMâMb<sup>â1</sup> in the left region (29â30âMb) and in two other regions in the same Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub> population. Recombination rates were also increased in the Gm Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> population (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig4">4b</a>). Similar increases were observed in the homoeologous D08 low-recombination haplotype blocks in the Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub> population. Moreover, these haplotype blocks of either parent segregated with expected ratios within the population of Gh Ã GmBC<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig13">9b</a>) or Gh Ã GtBC<sub>3</sub>F<sub>1</sub> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig13">9c</a>). These data suggest the stability and selection of these haplotype regions during domestication and breeding.</p><p>Notably, genome-wide recombination cold spots (haplotype block) and hotspots (no haplotype block) correlated with the DNA methylation frequency at CG, CHG (Hâ=âA, T or C) and CHH sites in the cultivated allotetraploids Gh and Gb (Pearson <i>r</i>â=â0.994; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig4">4c</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10a,b</a>), with higher methylation frequencies in the cold spots than in the hotspots (analysis of variance (ANOVA), <i>P</i>â&lt;â1-10e). The data support the role of DNA methylation in altering recombination landscapes, as reported in <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Mirouze, M. et al. Loss of DNA methylation affects the recombination landscape in Arabidopsis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 5880â5885 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2288">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Yelina, N. E. et al. DNA methylation epigenetically silences crossover hot spots and controls chromosomal domains of meiotic recombination in Arabidopsis. Genes Dev. 29, 2183â2202 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2291">52</a></sup>. Consistent with this notion, DNA methylation changes that are induced in the interspecific hybrid (Ga Ã Gr) are also largely maintained in the five allotetraploid cotton species, creating hundreds and possibly thousands of epialleles, including the ones responsible for photoperiodic flowering and worldwide cultivation of cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Song, Q., Zhang, T., Stelly, D. M. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Epigenomic and functional analyses reveal roles of epialleles in the loss of photoperiod sensitivity during domestication of allotetraploid cottons. Genome Biol. 18, 99 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2296">53</a></sup>.</p><p>Moreover, recombination events in all three interspecific crosses (Gb Ã GhF<sub>2</sub>, Gm Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> and Gt Ã GhBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub>) correlated negatively with the average numbers of strongly connecting sites (intensityâ&gt;â5) (<i>P</i>â&lt;â8.842âÃâ10<sup>â16</sup>) and their connection intensities (<i>P</i>â&lt;â7.26âÃâ10<sup>â12</sup>) of the Hi-C chromatin matrix (Pearson <i>r</i>â=ââ0.874; Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10c</a>). Recombination hotspots have fewer but more intense chromatin interactions within short distances, while the cold spots tend to have more but weaker interactions in long distances (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10c,d</a>). For example, 2 hotspots and 9 cold spots in the A08 region (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10d</a>), including 7 cold spots spanning ~32âMb correlated with weak Hi-C intensities and DNA hypermethylation (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig14">10e</a>). These data indicate that DNA hypermethylation and weak chromatin interactions interfere with recombination events in polyploid cottons.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Despite wide geographic distribution and diversification, five allotetraploid cotton genomes have largely retained the gene content and genomic synteny relative to respective extant diploids. This level of genome stability is in contrast to rapid genomic changes observed in some newly formed allotetraploids such as <i>B. napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7908â7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2351">10</a></sup> and <i>T. miscellus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Chester, M. et al. Extensive chromosomal variation in a recently formed natural allopolyploid species, Tragopogon miscellus (Asteraceae). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 109, 1176â1181 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2357">11</a></sup>. However, in cultivated canola, the two subgenomes are relatively undisrupted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950â953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2361">8</a></sup>, probably because the extant parental species existing today to make new tetraploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Xiong, Z., Gaeta, R. T. &amp; Pires, J. C. Homoeologous shuffling and chromosome compensation maintain genome balance in resynthesized allopolyploid Brassica napus. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 108, 7908â7913 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2365">10</a></sup> may be different from the ones that formed cultivated canola ~7,500 years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Lu, K. et al. Whole-genome resequencing reveals Brassica napus origin and genetic loci involved in its improvement. Nat. Commun. 10, 1154 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2370">16</a></sup> and likely became extinct. In addition, all five cotton polyploid species have a monophyletic origin, which is similar to the origin of wild and domesticated tetraploid peanuts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Yin, D. et al. Comparison of Arachis monticola with diploid and cultivated tetraploid genomes reveals asymmetric subgenome evolution and improvement of peanut. Adv. Sci. 7, 1901672 (2020)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2374">54</a></sup>, but different from recurrent formation of <i>Tragopogon</i> tetraploids<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Soltis, D. E. &amp; Soltis, P. S. Polyploidy: recurrent formation and genome evolution. Trends Ecol. Evol. 14, 348â352 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2381">55</a></sup>. Notably, since polyploid formation 1â1.5âMa, the evolution of 2 subgenomes in each of the 5 allotetraploid cotton species does not exhibit a simple asymmetrical pattern, as reported in Upland cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2385">25</a></sup>. Instead, the two subgenomes have diversified and experienced novel heterogeneous evolutionary trajectories, including partial equilibration of subgenome size mediated by differential TE exchanges, pervasive evolutionary rate shifts, and positive selection between homoeologs within and among lineages. These features present in all five allotetraploid species suggest that the âevolutionary tapeâ is replayed during polyploid diversification and speciation.</p><p>Among the five allotetraploid genomes, no species-specific orthologs were identified, except for one set of the unique genes related to fiber and seed traits in the two domesticated cottons and another set of the unique genes for reproduction and adaptation in the three wild polyploid species. However, R-gene families have rapidly evolved in each allotetraploid and extensively diversified during selection and domestication. These genomic diversifications have been accompanied by dynamic and prevalent gene expression changes during growth and development between wild and cultivated polyploid species, including parallel gene expression, coexpression networks and m<sup>6</sup>A mRNA modifications in fibers of the cultivated species. Remarkably, polyploid cotton genomes show recombination suppression or haplotype blocks, which correlate with altered epigenetic landscapes and can be overcome by wild introgression and possibly epigenetic manipulation. This finding is contemporary to the discovery of the <i>Ph1</i> locus that inhibits pairing of homoeologous chromosomes in polyploid wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Riley, R. &amp; Chapman, V. Genetic control of cytologically diploid behaviour of hexaploid wheat. Nature 182, 713â715 (1958)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2397">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Griffiths, S. et al. Molecular characterization of Ph1 as a major chromosome pairing locus in polyploid wheat. Nature 439, 749â752 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2400">57</a></sup>. The recombination suppression may help maintain a repository of epigenes or epialleles that were generated by interspecific hybridization accompanied by polyploidization and could have shaped polyploid cotton evolution, selection and domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Song, Q., Zhang, T., Stelly, D. M. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Epigenomic and functional analyses reveal roles of epialleles in the loss of photoperiod sensitivity during domestication of allotetraploid cottons. Genome Biol. 18, 99 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2404">53</a></sup>. These conceptual advances and genomic and epigenetic resources will help improve cotton fiber yield and quality as a sustainable alternative to petroleum-based synthetic fibers. Modifying epigenetic landscapes and using gene-editing tools may also overcome the limited genetic diversity within polyploid cottons. These principles may facilitate future efforts to concomitantly enhance the economic yield and sustainability of this global crop and possibly other polyploid crops.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Plant materials</h3><p><i>G. hirsutum</i> L. acc. TM-1 (1008001.06), <i>G. barbadense</i> L. acc. 3-79 (1400233.01), G. <i>tomentosum</i> L. (7179.01,02,03), <i>G. darwinii</i> L. (AD5-32, no. 1808015.09) and <i>G. mustelinum</i> L. (1408120.09, 1408120.10, 1408121.01, 1408121.02, 1408121.03) were grown in a greenhouse in College Station at Texas A&amp;M University. Young leaves were collected for preparation of high-molecular-weight DNA using a published method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Saski, C. A. et al. Sub genome anchored physical frameworks of the allotetraploid Upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.) genome, and an approach toward reference-grade assemblies of polyploids. Sci. Rep. 7, 15274 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2434">58</a></sup>. Total RNA was extracted from leaf, root, stem, square, cotyledon, hypocotyl, meristem, petal, stamen, exocarp, ovule (0, 3, 7, 14, 21 and 35âdays post anthesis (DPA)) and fiber (7, 14, 21 and 35âDPA) tissues in Gh; from leaf, root, stem, square, cotyledon, flower, ovule (14âDPA) and fiber (14âDPA) tissues in Gb; from leaf, root, stem, square, cotyledon and fiber (14âDPA) tissues in Gm; from leaf, root, stem, square, flower, ovule (0, 7, 14, 21 and 28âDPA) and fiber (7, 14, 21 and 28 DPA) tissues in Gt; and from leaf, root and stem tissues in Gd. Two or three biological replicates were used for RNA-seq and m<sup>6</sup>A RNA-seq analyses.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>Sequencing reads were collected using Illumina HiSeq and NovaSeq and PacBio SEQUEL and RSII platforms. We sequenced and assembled five <i>Gossypium</i> genomes using high-coverage (&gt;74Ã) single-molecule real-time long-read sequencing (Pac Biosciences). A total of six Illumina libraries were sequenced using the HiSeq platform, and two libraries were sequenced using NovaSeq. Initially, all five species were assembled using MECAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Xiao, C. L. et al. MECAT: fast mapping, error correction, and de novo assembly for single-molecule sequencing reads. Nat. Methods 14, 1072â1074 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2452">59</a></sup> and subsequently polished using long reads, as well as Illumina reads. Gb and Gh were polished using QUIVER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2456">60</a></sup>, while Gd, Gt and Gm were polished using ARROW<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Nonhybrid, finished microbial genome assemblies from long-read SMRT sequencing data. Nat. Methods 10, 563â569 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2460">60</a></sup>. Ten Hi-C libraries were sequenced for five cotton genomes (two for each species). The total amount of Illumina sequenced for all 5 species (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1</a>) is 4,361,212,302 reads for a total of 286.4Ã of high-quality Illumina bases. A total of 105,182,984 PacBio reads were sequenced for all 5 genomes with a total coverage of 439.61Ã.</p><p>Chromosome integration of Gb and Gh leveraged a combination of published Gh synteny and Hi-C scaffolding. A total of 148,239 unique, non-repetitive, non-overlapping 1-kb sequences were extracted from the published Gh genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Zhang, T. et al. Sequencing of allotetraploid cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L. acc. TM-1) provides a resource for fiber improvement. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 531â537 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2470">25</a></sup> and aligned to the Gh and Gb MECAT assemblies. Misjoins in the MECAT assembly were identified, and the assembly was scaffolded with Hi-C data using the JUICER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99â101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2474">61</a></sup>. Small rearrangements to both genomes were made using the JUICEBOX interface<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Robinson, J. T. et al. Juicebox.js provides a cloud-based visualization system for Hi-C data. Cell Syst. 6, 256â258.E1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2478">62</a></sup>. Finally, a set of 5,275 clones (474.3âMb total sequence) were used to patch remaining gaps in the Gh assembly. A total of 626 gaps were patched resulting in 1,871,050âbase pairs (bp) being added to the assembly. Gd and Gm were integrated into chromosomes using Gb (3-79) synteny, whereas Gt was integrated using the Gh release assembly version 1 <a href="https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Ghirsutum_er">https://phytozome.jgi.doe.gov/pz/portal.html#!info?alias=Org_Ghirsutum_er</a>. Final refinements to the Gt assembly were made using the JUICER/JUICEBOX pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Durand, N. C. et al. Juicebox provides a visualization system for Hi-C contact maps with unlimited zoom. Cell Syst. 3, 99â101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2486">61</a></sup>. In all five of the assemblies, care was taken to ensure that the telomere was properly oriented in the chromosomes, and the resulting sequence was screened for retained vector and/or contaminants. Genome annotation and gene prediction procedures are provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><p>Dot plots (pairwise comparisons) were generated using Gepard version 1.30 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Krumsiek, J., Arnold, R. &amp; Rattei, T. Gepard: a rapid and sensitive tool for creating dotplots on genome scale. Bioinformatics 23, 1026â1028 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2497">63</a></sup>). The input data consist of 2 FASTA files, as well as the appropriate flags (-seq1 FASTA_FILE_1 -seq2 FASTA_FILE_2 -matrix edna.mat -zoom 65000 -word 18 -lower 0 -upper 20 -greyscale 0 -format png), with the -zoom flag from 65,000 (D subgenome) to 119,000 (A subgenome). The edna.mat file is part of the Gepard version 1.30 release. As a rule of thumb, this factor is generated by dividing the number of bases of the input FASTA file by 1,000. The output from the Gepard command is a PNG image file.</p><p>Procedures for the analysis of SNPs and indels are provided in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Comparative analysis with published assemblies</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec16">Assessment of genome completeness</h4><p>We evaluated the genome assembly completeness by k-mer masking (24-nucleotide) reciprocally between Gh (TM-1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2519">22</a></sup> and Gh (TM-1, this study) and between Gb (Hai7124)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2523">22</a></sup> and Gb (3-79, this study). The unmasked contiguous sequences of the unshared sequence were extracted into a FASTA file and analyzed using FASTA statistics. BBMap (<a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap">https://sourceforge.net/projects/bbmap</a>) and Custom Python scripts (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>) were used for this analysis.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Genome comparisons using Hi-C data</h4><p>The Hi-C libraries IKCF (Gh) and ILDE (Gb) were aligned to published Gh and Gb reference genomes using BWA-MEM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;&#xA; (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2542">64</a></sup>. Heatmaps were generated using the JUICER-pre command, and visualized using JUICEBOX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Robinson, J. T. et al. Juicebox.js provides a cloud-based visualization system for Hi-C data. Cell Syst. 6, 256â258.E1 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2546">62</a></sup>. Inversions and rearrangements were further identified using JUICEBOX.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Analysis of chromosomal collinearity, structural rearrangements and gene family composition between reference assemblies</h4><p>Published Gh and Gb assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hu, Y. et al. Gossypium barbadense and Gossypium hirsutum genomes provide insights into the origin and evolution of allotetraploid cotton. Nat. Genet. 51, 739â748 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2558">22</a></sup> were aligned to the assemblies generated in this study using Minimap2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Li, H. Minimap2: pairwise alignment for nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 34, 3094â3100 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2562">65</a></sup>) with the parameter setting â-ax asm5 --eqxâ. The resulting alignments were used to identify structural rearrangements and local variations using SyRI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Goel, M., Sun, H., Jiao, W.-B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. Identification of syntenic and rearranged regions from whole-genome assemblies. Preprint at bioRxiv &#xA;https://doi.org/10.1101/546622&#xA;&#xA; (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2566">66</a></sup>. The gene copy numbers and gene families between assemblies were identified using OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2570">67</a></sup> based on all annotated protein-coding sequences.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Analysis of evolutionary rate changes and gene gain and loss</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">Evolutionary rate changes in subgenomes of allopolyploid cotton during diversification</h4><p>Rates of evolution for each subgenome of each species across the phylogeny were calculated using pairwise p-distances for the same 17,136 orthologs in all 5 polyploid species (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig8">4a</a>). The distribution of p-distances between each species was compared for both subgenomes using a one-tailed Wilcoxon signed rank test and Bonferroni correction for multiple testing. Differences in evolutionary rates between the subgenomes within each species were evaluated using a modified relative rate test whereby p-distance distributions were compared for both subgenomes to determine which had the greater p-distance (that is, higher inferred rate). Differences in subgenome evolutionary rates among lineages were estimated using a modified relative rate test that again used the Wilcoxon signed rank test with the p-distances of 17,136 genes, here comparing p-distances between two species relative to an outgroup species. This test was repeated for all possible pairs of tip and outgroup combinations. We also summed the total number of differences contained within all orthologs between each pairwise set of species, excluding all sites in which any of the orthologs contained a gap sequence (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">2a</a>). Chi-square tests were used to determine the significance of these total substitution counts (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">2b</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec21">Analysis of gene loss and gain after polyploid cotton formation</h4><p>A total of 32,622 groups of SCOs were identified between subgenomes of all 5 allopolyploids and the diploids Gr and Ga (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#Fig8">4c</a>). Of those, the 4,369 SCO groups that were present in both diploid species but absent in at least 1 allopolyploid subgenome were evaluated for gene losses specific to allopolyploids. The list of SCO groups was converted into a binary matrix of gene occurrence and mapped onto the inferred phylogeny of ten allopolyploid subgenomes (with five taxa each in the At- and Dt-subgenome clades, rooted by the respective diploid progenitors). Using a likelihoodâbased mixture model assuming predominantly gene losses over gains and stochastic mapping implemented in GLOOME<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Cohen, O. &amp; Pupko, T. Inference of gain and loss events from phyletic patterns using stochastic mapping and maximum parsimonyâa simulation study. Genome Biol. Evol. 3, 1265â1275 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2607">68</a></sup>, both the total number of gene gains and losses per branch and the associated probability of each event across the phylogeny were estimated.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec22">Identification of homoeologs under selection</h4><p>The homoeolog pairs of five species were used for estimating non-synonymous/synonymous (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) values. Every pair of the sequences were aligned using the MUSCLE alignment software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2627">69</a></sup> and then transferred to the AXT format for identifying positively selected genes (<i>K</i><sub>a</sub>/<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>â&gt;â1) using the KaKs calculator<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Wang, D., Zhang, Y., Zhang, Z., Zhu, J. &amp; Yu, J. KaKs_Calculator 2.0: a toolkit incorporating gamma-series methods and sliding window strategies. Genomics Proteomics Bioinformatics 8, 77â80 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2640">70</a></sup>. Positively selected genes in A and D homoeologs were compared pairwise among 5 species (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">2</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Analyses of repetitive sequences and TEs</h3><p>Pairwise comparison of 18-nucleotide sequences between homoeologous chromosomes was performed by Gepard plots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Krumsiek, J., Arnold, R. &amp; Rattei, T. Gepard: a rapid and sensitive tool for creating dotplots on genome scale. Bioinformatics 23, 1026â1028 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2657">63</a></sup>. Analysis of the <i>k</i>-mer content of all of the genomes was conducted by LTR-harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2664">71</a></sup> according to the manual. The whole-genome sequences were suffixed first and then indexed using the seed length 20. The frequency of individual 20-nucleotide sequences was estimated using in-house Perl scripts. This analysis was applied to the two diploid cotton species, Ga and Gr, and the five tetraploid allopolyploids, with the A or D subgenome examined separately. The software LTR-harvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2668">71</a></sup> and LTR-finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265âW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2672">72</a></sup> was used for identifying full-length LTR retrotransposons. The identification parameters were as follows. For LTR-harvest: overlaps best -seed 20 -minlenltr 100 -maxlenltr 2000 -mindistltr 3000 -maxdistltr 25000 -similar 85 -mintsd 4 -maxtsd 20 -motif tgca -motifmis 1 -vic 60 -xdrop 5 -mat 2 -mis -2 -ins -3 -del -3. For LTR-finder: -D 15000 -d 1000 -L 7000 -l 100 -p 20 -C -M 0.9. The two datasets were integrated to remove false positives using the LTR-retriever packages<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of long terminal repeat retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410â1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2677">73</a></sup>. The insertion time was estimated using the formula <i>T</i>â=â<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>/2<i>r</i>, where <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> is the divergence rate and <i>r</i> (3.48âÃâ10<sup>â9</sup>) is the substitution rate in cotton<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Grover, C. E. et al. Re-evaluating the phylogeny of allopolyploid Gossypium L. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 92, 45â52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2701">17</a></sup>.</p><p>Full-length TE sequences were extracted from each of the seven species and were used to build a TE database; the cd-hit software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Li, W. &amp; Godzik, A. Cd-hit: a fast program for clustering and comparing large sets of protein or nucleotide sequences. Bioinformatics 22, 1658â1659 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2708">74</a></sup> was applied to remove redundancies through self-sequence similarity tests, and sequences with identityâ&gt;â90% were grouped into the same cluster. A cluster present in only one species was defined as a species-specific TE cluster, and those present in more than one species were considered shared TE clusters. A total of 98,794 full-length LTRs were identified in all 7 cotton species and grouped into 20,583 clusters for analysis of their origins in Ga, Gr, and the A and D subgenomes in 5 allotetraploids.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">R-gene family and expression analysis in response to pathogen treatments</h3><p>We detected nucleotide-binding site, leucine-rich repeat (NBSâLRR) motifs with the pfamscan tool<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Chojnacki, S., Cowley, A., Lee, J., Foix, A. &amp; Lopez, R. Programmatic access to bioinformatics tools from EMBL-EBI update: 2017. Nucleic Acids Res. 45, W550âW553 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2720">75</a></sup> that uses the hidden Markov model search tool (HMMER) version 3.2.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2724">76</a></sup>) by searching primary protein-coding transcripts of each of the 5 allotetraploid cottons against the raw hidden Markov model for the NB-ARC-domain family downloaded from Pfam (PF00931). Identified NBSâLRR protein-coding genes for each of the allotetraploid cottons were further analyzed for amino-terminal (TIR/coiled-coil/other) and other functional domains by searching them against the Pfam-A hidden Markov model with the PfamScan tool and HMMER version 3.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Eddy, S. R. Accelerated profile HMM searches. PLoS Comput. Biol. 7, e1002195 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2728">76</a></sup>) with default settings (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Short-read sequencing data for bacterial blight were downloaded from the Sequence Read Archive from the NCBI Bioproject accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA395458">PRJNA395458</a> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Phillips, A. Z. et al. Genomics-enabled analysis of the emergent disease cotton bacterial blight. PLoS Genet. 13, e1007003 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2740">40</a></sup>). Reniform nematode sequence data were downloaded from the NCBI Bioproject accession <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA269348">PRJNA269348</a>. Sequence data were aligned to the 653 predicted R genes from the Gh version 2.0 (this study) with Bowtie2 version 2.3.4.1 and filtered for true-pair alignments. Fragments per kilobase million (FPKM) and read counts per million were determined with RSEM version 1.3.0. Differentially expressed R genes were determined with edgeR<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: a Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139â140 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2748">77</a></sup> using false discovery rate (FDR)-corrected <i>P</i> values of 0.05. Of the 291 A-subgenome and 384 D-subgenome predicted R genes, we found FPKM expression profiles (&gt;1) for at least 1 condition in 281 and 372 of the A- and D-subgenome predicted R genes, respectively. Similarly, in response to reniform nematode challenge in Gh, 274 of 291 A-subgenome and 370 of 384 D-subgenome predicted R genes were expressed at the FPKM level (&gt;1) for at least 1 of the 4 conditions tested.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">RNA-seq library construction, sequencing and data normalization</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from leaf, root, stem, square, flower, ovule and fiber samples from Gh, Gb, Gt, Gm and Gd species (2 replicates each for 124 samples; Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">9</a>), using PureLink Plant RNA Reagent (ThermoFisher). After DNase treatment, RNA-seq libraries were constructed using an NEBNext Ultra II RNA Library Kit (NEB), and 150-bp paired-end sequences were generated using an Illumina Hiseq 2500.</p><p>Paired-end sequence data were quality trimmed (<i>Q</i>ââ¥â25) and reads shorter than 50âbp after trimming were discarded. Sequences were then aligned to respective allotetraploid cotton genomes and counts of reads uniquely mapping to annotated genes were obtained using STAR (version 2.5.3a). Outliers among the biological replicates were verified on the basis of the Pearson correlation coefficient, <i>r</i><sup>2</sup>ââ¥â0.85. Fragments per kilobase of exon per million (FPKM) fragments mapped values were calculated for each gene by normalizing the read count data to both the length of the gene and the total number of mapped reads in the sample and considered as the metric for estimating gene expression levels<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Trapnell, C. et al. Differential gene and transcript expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with TopHat and Cufflinks. Nat. Protoc. 7, 562â578 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2776">78</a></sup>. Normalized count data were obtained using the relative logarithm expression (RLE) method in DESeq2 (version 1.14.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2780">79</a></sup>. Genes with low expression were filtered out, by requiring â¥2 RLE-normalized counts in at least 2 samples for each gene. Additional data for RNA-seq expression in fiber (28âDAP) tissue in both Gh and Gb were downloaded from the published data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Tuttle, J. R. et al. Metabolomic and transcriptomic insights into how cotton fiber transitions to secondary wall synthesis, represses lignification, and prolongs elongation. BMC Genomics 16, 477 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2784">44</a></sup> and processed as described above and in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Statistical analysis of differentially expressed genes</h3><p>To measure the gene expression differences between homoeologous genes in RNA-seq data, we used the DESeq2 package in R based on the negative binomial distribution (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Only genes with log<sub>2</sub>[fold change]ââ¥â1, BenjaminiâHochberg-adjusted <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05 were retained. The comparison of highly expressed homoeologous gene pairs between subgenomes in different tissues was carried out using a binomial test (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05). GO enrichment was analyzed using topGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Alexa, A. &amp; Rahenfuhrer, J. topGO: Enrichment analysis for Gene Ontology. R package version 2.32.0 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2811">80</a></sup>, an R Bioconductor package with Fisherâs exact test; only GO terms with <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05 (FDRâ&lt;â0.05) were considered significant.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Principal component analysis and correlation coefficient analysis</h3><p>To visualize subgenome and tissue expression relatedness, we used categorized gene expression values. These expression values were averaged across replicates and log<sub>2</sub>-transformed. Principal component analysis employed singular value decomposition via the prcomp function in R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2829">81</a></sup>. Categorized gene expression values were used in this analysis. Pearsonâs correlation coefficients were determined and hierarchical clustering was carried out using the Euclidian distance and complete linkage method.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">m<sup>6</sup>A RNA-seq data analysis</h3><p>m<sup>6</sup>A RNA-seq libraries were constructed using a modified protocol as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Dominissini, D., Moshitch-Moshkovitz, S., Salmon-Divon, M., Amariglio, N. &amp; Rechavi, G. Transcriptome-wide mapping of N&#xA;6-methyladenosine by m6A-seq based on immunocapturing and massively parallel sequencing. Nat. Protoc. 8, 176â189 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2846">82</a></sup>. Briefly, total RNA was extracted from young leaf and fiber tissues at 7âDPA (2 replicates each) from Gh by using PureLink Plant RNA Reagent (ThermoFisher). mRNA was collected from total RNA by the Oligotex mRNA mini kit (QIAGEN), fragmented and pulled down using an m<sup>6</sup>A antibody, followed by library construction using the NEBNext Ultra II RNA Library Kit (NEB) without polyA tail selection. Fragmented mRNA-seq libraries (control; input) and m<sup>6</sup>A RNA-seq libraries (IP) were sequenced using an Illumina Hiseq 2500 and 150-bp reads. Illumina reads were mapped to the Gh genome using Tophat 2.1.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2854">83</a></sup>), and the uniquely mapped reads were used to identify m<sup>6</sup>A peaks with the Bioconductor package exomePeak<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Meng, J., Cui, X. D., Rao, M. K., Chen, Y. D. &amp; Huang, Y. F. Exome-based analysis for RNA epigenome sequencing data. Bioinformatics 29, 1565â1567 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2861">84</a></sup> (Supplementary Dataset <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">12</a>).</p><p>GO terms were extracted from the GeneAnnotation_info.txt file. Identified m<sup>6</sup>A peak genes were analyzed by the Bioconductor package topGO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Alexa, A. &amp; Rahenfuhrer, J. topGO: Enrichment analysis for Gene Ontology. R package version 2.32.0 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2873">80</a></sup> to identify significantly over-represented GO terms (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.0001). The location of RNA (5Ê¹âUTR, CDS or 3Ê¹âUTR) for each m<sup>6</sup>A RNA-seq read (both input and IP) was identified using the intersect function of Bedtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841â842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2882">85</a></sup>. Single, double and triple asterisks indicate statistical significance levels of <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01 and <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001, respectively (Studentâs <i>t</i>-test).</p><p>We extracted the gene expression data for Gh leaf and fiber at 7âDPA corresponding to m<sup>6</sup>A peak genes. âAllâ refers to the expression level of all identified homoeologous genes in the leaf and fiber samples, while âpeakâ corresponds to the expression level of the identified m<sup>6</sup>A peaks for the genes in leaf (161 genes) and fiber (1,205 genes) samples. Single, double and triple asterisks indicate statistical significance levels of <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.05, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01 and <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001, respectively (Studentâs <i>t</i>-test).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Fluorescence in situ hybridization of A and D homoeologous chromosomes</h3><p>Procedures for the preparation of metaphase chromosomes in Gh and fluorescence in situ hybridization were adopted from a published protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Liu, B. &amp; Davis, T. M. Conservation and loss of ribosomal RNA gene sites in diploid and polyploid Fragaria (Rosaceae). BMC Plant Biol. 11, 157 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2928">86</a></sup>, with a modification that the cotton root tips were pretreated with cycloheximide (25âppm) for 3âh at room temperature. The 25S rDNA fragment was obtained from <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Unfried, I. &amp; Gruendler, P. Nucleotide sequence of the 5.8S and 25S rRNA genes and of the internal transcribed spacers from Arabidopsis thaliana. Nucleic Acids Res. 18, 4011 (1990)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2934">87</a></sup> and originally provided by R. Hasterok from Poland. Synthetic oligonucleotides for forward and reverse plant telomeric sequences were PCR-amplified and products were labeled by nick translation to create probe to detect telomeres<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Cox, A. V. et al. Comparison of plant telomere locations using a PCR-generated synthetic probe. Ann. Bot. 72, 239â247 (1993)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2938">88</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Genotyping and recombination rate analyses</h3><p>Genotyping data representing an improved cotton panel of 257 Gh accessions were acquired from a previously published diversity analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Hinze, L. L. et al. Diversity analysis of cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.) germplasm using the CottonSNP63K Array. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 37 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2950">49</a></sup> utilizing the CottonSNP63K array<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Hulse-Kemp, A. M. et al. Development of a 63K SNP array for cotton and high-density mapping of intraspecific and interspecific populations of Gossypium spp. Genes Genomes Genet. 5, 1187â1209 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2954">89</a></sup>. The genotyping data in 2 segregating populations included 18 lines each representing 1 family of a Gh Ã GmBC<sub>2</sub>F<sub>1</sub> population and 33 lines each representing 1 family of a Gh Ã GtBC<sub>3</sub>F<sub>1</sub> population. SNPs with a minor allele frequency greater than 5% and that had less than 10% missing data were retained. Genotyping data were further filtered for homeo-SNPs that occur due to intragenomic sequence identity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Hulse-Kemp, A. M. et al. Development of a 63K SNP array for cotton and high-density mapping of intraspecific and interspecific populations of Gossypium spp. Genes Genomes Genet. 5, 1187â1209 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2967">89</a></sup>. Array ID sequences were aligned to the Joint Genome Institute Gh version 2.0 sequence assembly using BLASTn<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Camacho, C. et al. BLAST plus: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10, 421 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2971">90</a></sup> (version 2.7.1+) with a minimum e-value cutoff of 1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>. Homoeologous alignments were corrected for using previously published SNP segregation data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Hulse-Kemp, A. M. et al. Development of a 63K SNP array for cotton and high-density mapping of intraspecific and interspecific populations of Gossypium spp. Genes Genomes Genet. 5, 1187â1209 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2977">89</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Ulloa, M., Hulse-Kemp, A. M., De Santiago, L. M., Stelly, D. M. &amp; Burke, J. J. Insights into upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.) genetic recombination based on 3 high-density single-nucleotide polymorphism and a consensus map developed independently with common parents. Genomics Insights 10, 1â15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2980">91</a></sup>, as well as interspecific, bi-parental linkage mapping populations from their respective Gh Ã GmBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> and Gh Ã GtBC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub> initial mapping populations. Genotyping data were then imputed and phased using Beagle (version 4.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Browning, S. R. &amp; Browning, B. L. Rapid and accurate haplotype phasing and missing-data inference for whole-genome association studies by use of localized haplotype clustering. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 1084â1097 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d11877e2993">92</a></sup>, and genotypes were converted to ABH format to distinguish genotypic parentage.</p><p>It is notable that erroneous SNP calling is a common problem in polyploids and especially in the AD-genome allotetraploid cotton because of homoeologous and paralogous sequences. This issue has been addressed through several methods<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Hulse-Kemp, A. M. et al. Development of a 63K SNP array for cotton and high-density mapping of intraspecific and interspecific populations of Gossypium spp. Genes Genomes Genet. 5, 1187â1209 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3000">89</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Korani, W., Clevenger, J. P., Chu, Y. &amp; Ozias-Akins, P. Machine learning as an effective method for identifying true single nucleotide polymorphisms in polyploid plants. Plant Genome 12, 180023 (2019)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3003">93</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Clevenger, J. P., Korani, W., Ozias-Akins, P. &amp; Jackson, S. Haplotype-based genotyping in polyploids. Front. Plant Sci. 9, 564 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3006">94</a></sup>. In this study, we used the published method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Hulse-Kemp, A. M. et al. Development of a 63K SNP array for cotton and high-density mapping of intraspecific and interspecific populations of Gossypium spp. Genes Genomes Genet. 5, 1187â1209 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3010">89</a></sup> to avoid erroneous genotype calling and to provide accurate chromosome-specific and homoeologous haplotype structure. Furthermore, we used a historical estimation of recombination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Gabriel, S. B. et al. The structure of haplotype blocks in the human genome. Science 296, 2225â2229 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3014">95</a></sup>, as shown in the haplotype structure using confidence intervals, as well as in two segregating populations, which led to the accurate estimates of recombination rates between parental alleles using linkage disequilibrium analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Gabriel, S. B. et al. The structure of haplotype blocks in the human genome. Science 296, 2225â2229 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3018">95</a></sup>. The haplotype block partitioning was conducted with PLINK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559â575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3022">50</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>).</p><p>The recombination map for chromosome A08 of Gh was developed using 4 SNP-based genetic maps, including 3 of interspecific crosses between Gb Ã Gh (F<sub>2</sub>, <i>n</i>â=â195), Gt Ã Gh (BC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub>, <i>n</i>â=â85) and Gm Ã Gh (BC<sub>1</sub>F<sub>1</sub>, <i>n</i>â=â59) and 1 consensus map that was generated using 3 intraspecific populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Ulloa, M., Hulse-Kemp, A. M., De Santiago, L. M., Stelly, D. M. &amp; Burke, J. J. Insights into upland cotton (Gossypium hirsutum L.) genetic recombination based on 3 high-density single-nucleotide polymorphism and a consensus map developed independently with common parents. Genomics Insights 10, 1â15 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3053">91</a></sup>. All genetic maps were aligned to the Joint Genome Institute Gh version 2.0 sequence assembly using the previously stated methods. Recombination map visualization was estimated using the R package MareyMap<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Rezvoy, C., Charif, D., Gueguen, L. &amp; Marais, G. A. B. MareyMap: an R-based tool with graphical interface for estimating recombination rates. Bioinformatics 23, 2188â2189 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3057">96</a></sup> using the nonlinear LOESS method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Cleveland, W. S. &amp; Grosse, E. Computational methods for local regression. Stat. Comput. 1, 47â62 (1991)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3061">97</a></sup>, and the number of surrounding markers used to fit a local polynomial was 7.5% of the total number of markers per chromosome. Final map plotting was conducted using the R package ggplot2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 98" title="Wickham, H. ggplot2: Elegant Graphics for Data Analysis (Springer, 2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR98" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3066">98</a></sup>). Localized recombination rates for chromosomes A08 and D08 were estimated using a 1-Mb non-overlapping sliding window with a minimum of 4 SNPs per window as a linear regression threshold using MareyMap.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">DNA methylation analysis</h3><p>Methylome sequencing data were downloaded from a published report<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Song, Q., Zhang, T., Stelly, D. M. &amp; Chen, Z. J. Epigenomic and functional analyses reveal roles of epialleles in the loss of photoperiod sensitivity during domestication of allotetraploid cottons. Genome Biol. 18, 99 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3078">53</a></sup>. In brief, methylC-seq reads of all allopolyploid cottons were mapped to genome sequences of Gh and Gb, respectively, using Bismark with the parameters (--score_min L,0,-0.2 -X 1000 --no-mixed --no-discordant)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 99" title="Krueger, F. &amp; Andrews, S. R. Bismark: a flexible aligner and methylation caller for Bisulfite-Seq applications. Bioinformatics 27, 1571â1572 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR99" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3082">99</a></sup>. Only the uniquely mapped reads were retained and used for further analysis. Reads mapped to the same site were collapsed into a single consensus molecule to reduce clonal bias. Cytosine counts were combined into 1,000-bp windows using methylKit 1.2.4 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 100" title="Akalin, A. et al. methylKit: a comprehensive R package for the analysis of genome-wide DNA methylation profiles. Genome Biol. 13, R87 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR100" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3086">100</a></sup>).</p><p>The DNA methylation (CG, CHG and CHH) levels (percentage of methylated cytosines) and average Hi-C seq statistics (number of connections, intensity or interaction matrix, and distance) in each recombination spot were compared using custom Python scripts. The Pearson correlation coefficient (<i>r</i>) was estimated using singular value decomposition via the prcomp function in R<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="R: A Language and Environment for Statistical Computing (R Foundation for Statistical Computing, 2018)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3096">81</a></sup>. Single, double and triple asterisks indicate statistical significance levels of <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001, <i>P</i>â&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup> and <i>P</i>â&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>, respectively, using one-way ANOVA.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec32">Chromatin conformation capture (Hi-C) sequencing analysis</h3><p>Hi-C seq libraries were constructed using a previously described protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 101" title="Belton, J. M. et al. Hi-C: a comprehensive technique to capture the conformation of genomes. Methods 58, 268â276 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR101" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3122">101</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 102" title="Louwers, M., Splinter, E., van Driel, R., de Laat, W. &amp; Stam, M. Studying physical chromatin interactions in plants using chromosome conformation capture (3C). Nat. Protoc. 4, 1216â1229 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR102" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3125">102</a></sup>, with modifications. Briefly, young leaves from Gh, Gb, Gt, Gm and Gd (2 replicates each) and fiber samples from Gh were fixed in 1% formaldehyde, and nuclei were extracted. Fixed chromatin was digested with DpnII, filled in using biotin-14-dATP and ligated. The biotin-labeled DNA was extracted and pulled down to construct HiC-seq libraries. Sequencing of Hi-C seq libraries was performed using an Illumina Hiseq 2500 and 150-bp reads. Reads were mapped to respective genomes and analyzed by HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 103" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#ref-CR103" id="ref-link-section-d11877e3129">103</a></sup>. The Hi-C read coverage is 205Ã for Gh, 45Ã for Gb, 36Ã for Gm, 22Ã for Gd and 17Ã for Gt. The Hi-C data were largely used to correct orientations and misalignments in the assemblies of contigs and scaffolds. For Gh, Hi-C data were used to generate chromatin connection heatmaps with the HiCPlotter (<a href="https://github.com/kcakdemir/HiCPlotter">https://github.com/kcakdemir/HiCPlotter</a>). Single, double and triple asterisks indicate statistical significance levels of <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001, <i>P</i>â&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â5</sup> and <i>P</i>â&lt;â1âÃâ10<sup>â10</sup>, respectively, using one-way ANOVA.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec33">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM2">Nature Genetics Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>Sequencing data are accessible under NCBI BioProject numbers (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA515894">PRJNA515894</a> for Gh, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA516412">PRJNA516412</a> for Gt, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA516411">PRJNA516411</a> for Gb, <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA516409">PRJNA516409</a> for Gd and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA525892">PRJNA525892</a> for Gm). All datasets generated and/or analyzed in this study are available in the Article, the Source Data files that accompany Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM4">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM7">4</a> and Extended Data Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM8">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM17">10</a>, Supplementary Datasets <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM3">12</a>, the Reporting Summary or the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>. Additional data such as raw image files that support this study are available from the corresponding authors upon request.</p>

                        </p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig12"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41588-020-0614-5/figures/12" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-020-0614-5/MediaObjects/41588_2020_614_Fig12_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 8 Gene Ontology (GO) analysis of differentially expressed genes and analysis of m<sup>6</sup>A mRNA modifications in Upland cotton.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p><b>a</b>, GO analysis of upregulated genes in two cultivated cottons and three wild relatives (&gt;2-fold change, FPKMâ&gt;â5, and ANOVA p-value &lt; 0.05) and m<sup>6</sup>A-associated genes in the leaf and fiber of Upland cotton.
